The Definition of Certainty in Demonstration
Author defines certainty and its conditions in demonstration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus and differentia in the definition of yaqin and distinguish it from mere belief based on the author's conditions.

Core Idea
Certainty is knowledge that excludes even the possibility of error.

In this passage, the author gives a precise definition of certainty (yaqin) as it applies to demonstrative proofs. He insists that certainty is not just any conviction, but one that comes with the absolute conviction that the thing cannot be otherwise. Understanding this definition is crucial because it sets the standard for what counts as a valid demonstration in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The author defines 'اليقين' (certainty). Locate the sentence where he says 'العلم اليقيني هو أن تعرف أن الشيء بصفة كذا مقترناً بالتصديق بأنه لا يمكن أن لا يكون كذا'.
This is the core definition.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus is 'العلم' (knowledge). The differentia is 'بأنه لا يمكن أن لا يكون كذا' (that it cannot be otherwise). Notice the condition that any possibility of error or forgetfulness negates certainty.
The differentia includes a negation of possibility of otherness.
3
Test the definition's completeness
Check if the definition includes all instances of certainty and excludes all non-instances. The author adds that even if the contrary is attributed to a prophet, you must reject it or reinterpret it – this shows the strength of the differentia.
This test shows the definition is hadd haqiqi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيان اليقين بحسب مقدمات البرهان الحقيقي ما يفيد شيئاً لا يتصوّر تغيره، ويكون ذلك البرهان فإنها تكون يقينية أبدية لا تستحيل ولا تتغير أبداً، وأعني بذلك أن الشيء لا يتغير وإن غفل إنسان عنه كقولنا : الكلّ أعظم من الجزء والأشياء المساوية لشيء واحد متساوية وأمثالها فالنتيجة الحاصلة منها أيضاً تكون يقينية، والعلم اليقيني هو أن تعرف أن الشيء بصفة كذا مقترناً بالتصديق بأنه لا يمكن أن لا يكون كذا، فإنك لو خطر ببالك إمكان الخطأ فيه والذهول عنه لم ينقدح لم ينقدح ذلك في نفسك أصلاً، فإن اقترن به تجوّز الخطأ وإمكانه فليس بيقيني؛ فهكذا ينبغي نتائج البرهان فإن عرفته معرفة على حد قولنا فقيل لك خلافه حكاية عن أعظم خلق الله مرتبة وأجلّهم في النظر والعقليات درجة، وأورث ذلك عندك احتمالاً، فليس اليقين تاماً، بل لو نقل عن نبي صادق نقيضه فينبغي أن يقطع بكذب الناقل أو بتأويل اللفظ المسموع عنه، ولا يخطر ببالك إمكان الصدق، فإن لم يقبل التأويل فشك في نبوة من حكى عنه، بخلاف ما عقلت إن كان ما عقلته يقيناً فإن شككت في صدقه لم يكن يقينك تاما . فإن قلت : ربما ظهر لي برهان صدقه ثم سمعت منه ما يناقض برهاناً قام عندي. فأقول : وجود هذا يستحيل كقول القائل : لو تناقضت الأخبار المتواترة فما السبيل فيها كما لو تواتر وجود مكة وعدمها ؟ فهذا محال فالتناقض في البراهين الجامعة للشروط التي ذكرناها محال فإن رأيتها متناقضة فاعلم أن أحدهما أو كلاهما لم تتحقق فيه الشروط المذكورة فتفقد مظانّ الغلط والمثارات السبع التي فصلناها، وأكثر الغلط يكون في المبادرة إلى تسليم مقدمات البرهان على أنها أولية ولا تكون أولية بل ربما تكون محمودة مشهورة أو وهمية، ولا ينبغي أن تسلم المقدمات ما لم يكن اليقين فيها على الحد الذي وصفناه وكما يظنّ فيما ليست أولية أنها أولية فقط يظنّ بالأوليات أنها ليست أولية فيشكك فيها، ولا يتشكك في الأوليات إلّا بزوال الذهن عن الفطرة السليمة لمخالطة بعض المتكلمين المتعصبين للمذاهب الفاسدة بمجاحدة الجليّات حتى تأنس النفس بسماعها فيشك في اليقيني، كما أنه قد يتكرّر على سمعه ما ليس يقيناً من المحمودات فتذعن للتصديق به وتظنّ أنه يقيني بكثرة سماعه، وهذا أعظم مثارات الغلط ويعزّ في العقلاء من يحسن الاحتراز من الاغترار به . فإن قلت : فمثل هذا اليقين عزيز يقل وجوده فتقل به المقدمات . قلنا: ما يتساعد فيه الوهم والعقل من الحسابيات والهندسيات والحسّيات كثير، فيكثر فيها مثل هذه اليقينيات وكذا المعقولات التي لا تحاذيها الوهميات . فأما العقليات الصرفة المتعلقة بالنظر في الإلهيات ففيها بعض مثل هذه اليقينيات، ولا يبلغ اليقين فيها إلى الحد الذي ذكرناه إلا بطول ممارسة العقليات وفطام العقل عن الوهميات والحسّيات وإيناسها بالعقليات المحضة، وكلما كان النظر فيها أكثر والجد في طلبها أتمّ كانت المعارف فيها إلى حدّ اليقين التام أقرب، ثم من طالت ممارسته وحصلت له ملكة بتلك المعارف لا يقدر على إفحام الخصم فيه ولا يقدر على تنزيل المسترشد منزلة نفسه بمجرد ذكر ما عنده إلا بأن يرشده إلى أن يسلك مسلكه في ممارسة العلوم وطول التأمل، حتى يصل إلى ما وصل إليه إن كان الحدس ثاقب العقل صافي الذكاء، وإن فارقه في الذكاء أو في الحدس أو تولّي الاعتبار الذي تولاه لم يصل إلى ما وصل إليه، وعند ذلك يقابل ما يحكيه
Chapter on the Explanation of Certainty: According to the premises of true demonstration, it yields something that cannot be conceived of as changing, and that demonstration is such that its premises are certain and eternal, not subject to change or alteration ever. I mean by that that a thing does not change even if a person is heedless of it, as when we say: 'The whole is greater than the part' and 'Things equal to the same thing are equal' and the like. So the conclusion derived from them is also certain. Certain knowledge is that you know a thing to have a certain property, accompanied by the conviction that it cannot be otherwise. For if the possibility of error or forgetfulness occurs to your mind, that conviction does not arise in your soul at all. If it is accompanied by a consideration of error and its possibility, then it is not certain. Thus should the conclusions of demonstration be. If you know it in the manner we described, and then it is said to you contrary to it, narrated from the greatest of Allah's creation in rank and the most eminent in theoretical and rational matters, and that gives rise to some possibility in you, then your certainty is not complete. Rather, even if the opposite were narrated from a truthful prophet, you should judge the narrator to be a liar or interpret the words heard from him, and the possibility of truth should not enter your mind. If it cannot be interpreted, then you doubt the prophethood of the one from whom it was narrated. Contrary to what you have understood: if what you understood is certain, and you doubt his veracity, then your certainty is not complete. If you say: 'Perhaps a proof of his truthfulness has appeared to me, but then I heard from him something contradictory to a proof established in my mind.' I say: The existence of this is impossible, like one who says: 'If contradictory reports were widespread, what would be the way out? Just as if it were reported by tawatur that Mecca exists and that it does not exist.' This is impossible. So contradiction in demonstrations that fulfill the conditions we have mentioned is impossible. If you see them contradictory, know that one or both of them have not satisfied the mentioned conditions. So examine the sources of error and the seven provocations that we have detailed. Most error occurs in rushing to accept premises of demonstration as if they are primary, when they are not primary but perhaps are commendable, widely accepted, or illusory. One should not accept premises unless certainty in them is at the level we described. And just as one thinks that things that are not primary are primary, so too one thinks that primary things are not primary, and thus one becomes skeptical of them. No one becomes skeptical of primary things except by the mind departing from sound innate disposition due to mixing with certain disputatious theologians of corrupt sects, who deny self-evident truths until the soul becomes accustomed to hearing them and thus doubts the certain. Likewise, what is not certain of commendable statements may be repeated to his hearing until he submits to believing it and thinks it is certain due to its frequent hearing. This is the greatest source of error, and rare among the rational is one who can guard against being deceived by it. If you say: 'Such certainty is rare, so premises become rare.' We say: Where the imagination and intellect agree, such as in the mathematical, geometrical, and perceptual sciences, there are many such certainties, as well as the intelligibles that have no corresponding imaginables. As for pure intelligibles related to investigation in the divine sciences, there are some such certainties in them, but certainty reaches the level we have described only through long practice of the intellect and weaning the intellect from imaginables and sensibles, and familiarizing it with pure intelligibles. The more one studies them and the more diligent one is in seeking them, the closer one's knowledge in them approaches the level of complete certainty. Then, when one has long practiced and acquired a faculty through those sciences, he is not able to refute his opponent nor to bring the seeker of guidance to his own level merely by mentioning what he has, unless he guides him to follow his same path of practicing the sciences and long reflection, until he reaches what he has reached, provided that the seeker has penetrating intuition and pure intelligence. But if he differs in intelligence or intuition, or if he does not undertake the consideration that he undertook, he will not reach what he reached. At that point, what he narrates confronts...
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological treatise on the nature of certainty (yaqin) in demonstrative proofs (burhan). The author defines true demonstration as yielding certainty that is eternal and unchangeable, using examples like 'the whole is greater than the part' and 'things equal to the same thing are equal'. Certain knowledge is defined as knowing something with the conviction that it cannot be otherwise — if any shadow of doubt or possibility of error remains, it is not certainty. The author emphasizes that even if the contrary is attributed to the most learned person, or even to a truthful prophet, you must reject the report or interpret it allegorically; otherwise, your certainty is flawed. Then an objection is raised: what if a proof of his truthfulness conflicts with another proof? The response is that true demonstrations cannot contradict each other; if apparent contradiction occurs, one or both proofs lack the required conditions. The author identifies the greatest source of error: prematurely accepting premises as self-evident (awwaliyyat) when they are not, or doubting genuine self-evident truths due to exposure to sophistical arguments from biased theologians. Also, frequently repeated but uncertain statements can be mistaken for certain. The passage concludes by acknowledging that such rigorous certainty is rare, but it is abundant in mathematical and perceptual sciences. In pure rational sciences (like theology), it requires extensive practice, intellectual discipline, and weaning from imagination. Achieving such certainty may not allow one to convince opponents directly; rather, one must guide them to follow the same path of study. This passage reflects the Ash'ari or Avicennan emphasis on the primacy of certain knowledge in demonstration and the rejection of skepticism.

Key Terms
اليقين
البرهان
الأوليات
المحمودات
الحسيات

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what should you do if a reported statement from a truthful prophet seems to contradict a certain truth?
A) Accept both as equally certain.
B) Reject the report as false or reinterpret it.
C) Doubt your own certainty.
D) Treat the report as allegorical while maintaining your certainty.

Key Takeaway
Certainty is knowledge accompanied by the absolute conviction that it cannot be otherwise, and any possibility of error destroys it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Demonstration -- Burhan

Why True Demonstrations Cannot Contradict Each Other
Author argues that genuine demonstrations cannot contradict each other.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument that genuine demonstrations cannot contradict each other and identify the conditions that ensure certainty.

Core Idea
If two demonstrations seem contradictory, at least one fails the conditions of certainty.

The author anticipates an objection: what if a certain proof of a prophet's truthfulness seems to contradict another certain proof? His response is firm: true demonstrations cannot conflict. This lesson unpacks the logical structure of that argument and shows how it protects the integrity of demonstrative knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The hypothetical objector says: 'Perhaps a proof of his truthfulness appears to me, but then I hear from him something that contradicts a proof established in my mind.' Locate the phrase 'فإن قلت: ربما ظهر لي برهان صدقه...'
This sets up a potential conflict between two proofs.
2
Follow the author's response
The author declares this impossible, comparing it to a tawatur report that Mecca both exists and does not. He says: 'Contradiction in demonstrations that fulfill the conditions we have mentioned is impossible.'
The response is a categorical denial of the possibility.
3
Reconstruct the reasoning form
The argument is: If two demonstrations are both genuine (fulfill conditions), their conclusions will be certain and cannot contradict. If contradiction appears, then at least one demonstration lacks the conditions. This is a conditional argument: if P then Q, not Q, therefore not P. (Modus tollens)
The objector's scenario assumes two proofs seeming to contradict, but the author says that disproves their genuineness.
4
Verify the conditions for certainty
Check what conditions ensure certainty: premises must be self-evident (awwaliyyat) or derived from certain sources, and the argument must be valid. The author implies that if both met these, contradiction is impossible.
These conditions are earlier in the passage: premises are certain and eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيان اليقين بحسب مقدمات البرهان الحقيقي ما يفيد شيئاً لا يتصوّر تغيره، ويكون ذلك البرهان فإنها تكون يقينية أبدية لا تستحيل ولا تتغير أبداً، وأعني بذلك أن الشيء لا يتغير وإن غفل إنسان عنه كقولنا : الكلّ أعظم من الجزء والأشياء المساوية لشيء واحد متساوية وأمثالها فالنتيجة الحاصلة منها أيضاً تكون يقينية، والعلم اليقيني هو أن تعرف أن الشيء بصفة كذا مقترناً بالتصديق بأنه لا يمكن أن لا يكون كذا، فإنك لو خطر ببالك إمكان الخطأ فيه والذهول عنه لم ينقدح لم ينقدح ذلك في نفسك أصلاً، فإن اقترن به تجوّز الخطأ وإمكانه فليس بيقيني؛ فهكذا ينبغي نتائج البرهان فإن عرفته معرفة على حد قولنا فقيل لك خلافه حكاية عن أعظم خلق الله مرتبة وأجلّهم في النظر والعقليات درجة، وأورث ذلك عندك احتمالاً، فليس اليقين تاماً، بل لو نقل عن نبي صادق نقيضه فينبغي أن يقطع بكذب الناقل أو بتأويل اللفظ المسموع عنه، ولا يخطر ببالك إمكان الصدق، فإن لم يقبل التأويل فشك في نبوة من حكى عنه، بخلاف ما عقلت إن كان ما عقلته يقيناً فإن شككت في صدقه لم يكن يقينك تاما . فإن قلت : ربما ظهر لي برهان صدقه ثم سمعت منه ما يناقض برهاناً قام عندي. فأقول : وجود هذا يستحيل كقول القائل : لو تناقضت الأخبار المتواترة فما السبيل فيها كما لو تواتر وجود مكة وعدمها ؟ فهذا محال فالتناقض في البراهين الجامعة للشروط التي ذكرناها محال فإن رأيتها متناقضة فاعلم أن أحدهما أو كلاهما لم تتحقق فيه الشروط المذكورة فتفقد مظانّ الغلط والمثارات السبع التي فصلناها، وأكثر الغلط يكون في المبادرة إلى تسليم مقدمات البرهان على أنها أولية ولا تكون أولية بل ربما تكون محمودة مشهورة أو وهمية، ولا ينبغي أن تسلم المقدمات ما لم يكن اليقين فيها على الحد الذي وصفناه وكما يظنّ فيما ليست أولية أنها أولية فقط يظنّ بالأوليات أنها ليست أولية فيشكك فيها، ولا يتشكك في الأوليات إلّا بزوال الذهن عن الفطرة السليمة لمخالطة بعض المتكلمين المتعصبين للمذاهب الفاسدة بمجاحدة الجليّات حتى تأنس النفس بسماعها فيشك في اليقيني، كما أنه قد يتكرّر على سمعه ما ليس يقيناً من المحمودات فتذعن للتصديق به وتظنّ أنه يقيني بكثرة سماعه، وهذا أعظم مثارات الغلط ويعزّ في العقلاء من يحسن الاحتراز من الاغترار به . فإن قلت : فمثل هذا اليقين عزيز يقل وجوده فتقل به المقدمات . قلنا: ما يتساعد فيه الوهم والعقل من الحسابيات والهندسيات والحسّيات كثير، فيكثر فيها مثل هذه اليقينيات وكذا المعقولات التي لا تحاذيها الوهميات . فأما العقليات الصرفة المتعلقة بالنظر في الإلهيات ففيها بعض مثل هذه اليقينيات، ولا يبلغ اليقين فيها إلى الحد الذي ذكرناه إلا بطول ممارسة العقليات وفطام العقل عن الوهميات والحسّيات وإيناسها بالعقليات المحضة، وكلما كان النظر فيها أكثر والجد في طلبها أتمّ كانت المعارف فيها إلى حدّ اليقين التام أقرب، ثم من طالت ممارسته وحصلت له ملكة بتلك المعارف لا يقدر على إفحام الخصم فيه ولا يقدر على تنزيل المسترشد منزلة نفسه بمجرد ذكر ما عنده إلا بأن يرشده إلى أن يسلك مسلكه في ممارسة العلوم وطول التأمل، حتى يصل إلى ما وصل إليه إن كان الحدس ثاقب العقل صافي الذكاء، وإن فارقه في الذكاء أو في الحدس أو تولّي الاعتبار الذي تولاه لم يصل إلى ما وصل إليه، وعند ذلك يقابل ما يحكيه
Chapter on the Explanation of Certainty: According to the premises of true demonstration, it yields something that cannot be conceived of as changing, and that demonstration is such that its premises are certain and eternal, not subject to change or alteration ever. I mean by that that a thing does not change even if a person is heedless of it, as when we say: 'The whole is greater than the part' and 'Things equal to the same thing are equal' and the like. So the conclusion derived from them is also certain. Certain knowledge is that you know a thing to have a certain property, accompanied by the conviction that it cannot be otherwise. For if the possibility of error or forgetfulness occurs to your mind, that conviction does not arise in your soul at all. If it is accompanied by a consideration of error and its possibility, then it is not certain. Thus should the conclusions of demonstration be. If you know it in the manner we described, and then it is said to you contrary to it, narrated from the greatest of Allah's creation in rank and the most eminent in theoretical and rational matters, and that gives rise to some possibility in you, then your certainty is not complete. Rather, even if the opposite were narrated from a truthful prophet, you should judge the narrator to be a liar or interpret the words heard from him, and the possibility of truth should not enter your mind. If it cannot be interpreted, then you doubt the prophethood of the one from whom it was narrated. Contrary to what you have understood: if what you understood is certain, and you doubt his veracity, then your certainty is not complete. If you say: 'Perhaps a proof of his truthfulness has appeared to me, but then I heard from him something contradictory to a proof established in my mind.' I say: The existence of this is impossible, like one who says: 'If contradictory reports were widespread, what would be the way out? Just as if it were reported by tawatur that Mecca exists and that it does not exist.' This is impossible. So contradiction in demonstrations that fulfill the conditions we have mentioned is impossible. If you see them contradictory, know that one or both of them have not satisfied the mentioned conditions. So examine the sources of error and the seven provocations that we have detailed. Most error occurs in rushing to accept premises of demonstration as if they are primary, when they are not primary but perhaps are commendable, widely accepted, or illusory. One should not accept premises unless certainty in them is at the level we described. And just as one thinks that things that are not primary are primary, so too one thinks that primary things are not primary, and thus one becomes skeptical of them. No one becomes skeptical of primary things except by the mind departing from sound innate disposition due to mixing with certain disputatious theologians of corrupt sects, who deny self-evident truths until the soul becomes accustomed to hearing them and thus doubts the certain. Likewise, what is not certain of commendable statements may be repeated to his hearing until he submits to believing it and thinks it is certain due to its frequent hearing. This is the greatest source of error, and rare among the rational is one who can guard against being deceived by it. If you say: 'Such certainty is rare, so premises become rare.' We say: Where the imagination and intellect agree, such as in the mathematical, geometrical, and perceptual sciences, there are many such certainties, as well as the intelligibles that have no corresponding imaginables. As for pure intelligibles related to investigation in the divine sciences, there are some such certainties in them, but certainty reaches the level we have described only through long practice of the intellect and weaning the intellect from imaginables and sensibles, and familiarizing it with pure intelligibles. The more one studies them and the more diligent one is in seeking them, the closer one's knowledge in them approaches the level of complete certainty. Then, when one has long practiced and acquired a faculty through those sciences, he is not able to refute his opponent nor to bring the seeker of guidance to his own level merely by mentioning what he has, unless he guides him to follow his same path of practicing the sciences and long reflection, until he reaches what he has reached, provided that the seeker has penetrating intuition and pure intelligence. But if he differs in intelligence or intuition, or if he does not undertake the consideration that he undertook, he will not reach what he reached. At that point, what he narrates confronts...

Demonstration -- Burhan

The Greatest Sources of Error in Accepting Premises
Author identifies three common errors in evaluating premises for certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify three types of errors in accepting premises: mistaking non-axioms for axioms, doubting genuine axioms due to bias, and being influenced by frequent repetition of uncertain statements.

Core Idea
Most error in reasoning stems from rushing to accept premises that are not truly certain.

After establishing the strict conditions for certainty, the author warns about common pitfalls. He identifies three major sources of error: treating commendable or illusory statements as self-evident, doubting self-evident truths due to exposure to sophistical theologians, and mistaking frequently heard statements for certain truths. This lesson helps you recognize these fallacies in your own thinking and in arguments you encounter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first source of error
The author says: 'Most error occurs in rushing to accept premises of demonstration as if they are primary, when they are not primary but perhaps are commendable, widely accepted, or illusory.' Look for where he mentions 'المبادرة إلى تسليم مقدمات البرهان على أنها أولية ولا تكون أولية'.
This is the error of accepting non-axioms as axioms.
2
Identify the second source of error
The opposite error: doubting genuine primary truths due to mixing with disputatious theologians who deny self-evident truths. The author says: 'No one becomes skeptical of primary things except by the mind departing from sound innate disposition due to mixing with certain disputatious theologians of corrupt sects.'
This shows that bias can lead to false skepticism.
3
Identify the third source of error
The error of accepting frequently repeated statements as certain: 'What is not certain of commendable statements may be repeated to his hearing until he submits to believing it and thinks it is certain due to its frequent hearing.'
Repetition can create false certainty.
4
Classify each error as a fallacy type
The first is a fallacy of false assumption (taking something as self-evident when it is not). The second is a fallacy of biased skepticism. The third is a fallacy of appeal to frequency. Recognising these helps avoid them.
These are not formal logical fallacies but epistemological errors.

Most error occurs in rushing to accept premises of demonstration as if they are primary, when they are not primary but perhaps are commendable, widely accepted, or illusory. One should not accept premises unless certainty in them is at the level we described. And just as one thinks that things that are not primary are primary, so too one thinks that primary things are not primary, and thus one becomes skeptical of them. No one becomes skeptical of primary things except by the mind departing from sound innate disposition due to mixing with certain disputatious theologians of corrupt sects, who deny self-evident truths until the soul becomes accustomed to hearing them and thus doubts the certain. Likewise, what is not certain of commendable statements may be repeated to his hearing until he submits to believing it and thinks it is certain due to its frequent hearing. This is the greatest source of error, and rare among the rational is one who can guard against being deceived by it. If you say: 'Such certainty is rare, so premises become rare.' We say: Where the imagination and intellect agree, such as in the mathematical, geometrical, and perceptual sciences, there are many such certainties, as well as the intelligibles that have no corresponding imaginables. As for pure intelligibles related to investigation in the divine sciences, there are some such certainties in them, but certainty reaches the level we have described only through long practice of the intellect and weaning the intellect from imaginables and sensibles, and familiarizing it with pure intelligibles. The more one studies them and the more diligent one is in seeking them, the closer one's knowledge in them approaches the level of complete certainty. Then, when one has long practiced and acquired a faculty through those sciences, he is not able to refute his opponent nor to bring the seeker of guidance to his own level merely by mentioning what he has, unless he guides him to follow his same path of practicing the sciences and long reflection, until he reaches what he has reached, provided that the seeker has penetrating intuition and pure intelligence. But if he differs in intelligence or intuition, or if he does not undertake the consideration that he undertook, he will not reach what he reached. At that point, what he narrates confronts...
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological treatise on the nature of certainty (yaqin) in demonstrative proofs (burhan). The author defines true demonstration as yielding certainty that is eternal and unchangeable, using examples like 'the whole is greater than the part' and 'things equal to the same thing are equal'. Certain knowledge is defined as knowing something with the conviction that it cannot be otherwise — if any shadow of doubt or possibility of error remains, it is not certainty. The author emphasizes that even if the contrary is attributed to the most learned person, or even to a truthful prophet, you must reject the report or interpret it allegorically; otherwise, your certainty is flawed. Then an objection is raised: what if a proof of his truthfulness conflicts with another proof? The response is that true demonstrations cannot contradict each other; if apparent contradiction occurs, one or both proofs lack the required conditions. The author identifies the greatest source of error: prematurely accepting premises as self-evident (awwaliyyat) when they are not, or doubting genuine self-evident truths due to exposure to sophistical arguments from biased theologians. Also, frequently repeated but uncertain statements can be mistaken for certain. The passage concludes by acknowledging that such rigorous certainty is rare, but it is abundant in mathematical and perceptual sciences. In pure rational sciences (like theology), it requires extensive practice, intellectual discipline, and weaning from imagination. Achieving such certainty may not allow one to convince opponents directly; rather, one must guide them to follow the same path of study. This passage reflects the Ash'ari or Avicennan emphasis on the primacy of certain knowledge in demonstration and the rejection of skepticism.

The Impossibility of Contradiction in Genuine Demonstrations
Author refutes the possibility of contradiction between true demonstrations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to diagnose whether an apparent contradiction between two arguments indicates a flaw in one or both demonstrations, using the author's principle that real demonstrations cannot contradict.

Core Idea
Real demonstrations never contradict; apparent contradiction reveals a missing condition.

This passage from the classical text tackles a crucial objection: if a person has a demonstration for a prophet's truthfulness, but then hears something from that prophet that seems to contradict another demonstration, does that not imply a genuine contradiction between demonstrations? The author argues it is impossible, and the error lies in accepting premises that are not truly certain. Understanding this principle is essential for maintaining consistency in theological and philosophical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's scenario
Find the hypothetical situation the objector presents: having a demonstration for a prophet's truthfulness, then hearing something from that prophet that contradicts another demonstration.
This scenario is designed to test whether contradictions can arise between genuine demonstrations.
2
Locate the author's response
The author states: 'The existence of this is impossible.' He uses an analogy with mutawatir reports: just as it is impossible for mutawatir reports about Mecca's existence and non-existence to both be true, so too for demonstrations meeting all conditions.
The analogy emphasizes that when conditions are fully satisfied, contradiction is logically impossible.
3
Extract the key condition
The author says contradiction is impossible 'in demonstrations that include the conditions we have mentioned.' This refers to conditions from prior lessons: premises must be primary or derived from them, and the form must be valid.
Understanding these conditions is necessary to apply the principle correctly.
4
Learn the diagnostic principle
If you see contradiction, know that one or both demonstrations 'did not fulfill the mentioned conditions.' Then examine the seven sources of error, especially rushing to accept premises as primary when they are actually well-known or imaginary.
This principle turns an apparent contradiction into a clue for detecting error.

Source Text

التأويل فشك في نبوة من حكى عنه، بخلاف ما عقلت إن كان ما عقلته يقيناً فإن شككت في صدقه لم يكن يقينك تاما
·
فإن قلت: ربما ظهر لي برهان صدقه ثم سمعت منه ما يناقض برهاناً قام عندي. فأقول: وجود هذا يستحيل كقول القائل: لو تناقضت الأخبار المتواترة فما السبيل فيها كما لو تواتر وجود مكة وعدمها؟ فهذا محال فالتناقض في البراهين الجامعة للشروط التي ذكرناها محال فإن رأيتها متناقضة فاعلم أن أحدهما أو كلاهما لم تتحقق فيه الشروط المذكورة فتفقد مظانّ الغلط والمثارات السبع التي فصلناها، وأكثر الغلط يكون في المبادرة إلى تسليم مقدمات البرهان على أنها أولية ولا تكون أولية بل ربما تكون محمودة مشهورة أو وهمية، ولا ينبغي أن تسلم المقدمات ما لم يكن اليقين فيها على الحد الذي وصفناه وكما يظنّ فيما ليست أولية أنها أولية فقط يظنّ بالأوليات أنها ليست أولية فيشكك فيها، ولا يتشكك في الأوليات إلّا بزوال الذهن عن الفطرة السليمة لمخالطة بعض المتكلمين المتعصبين للمذاهب الفاسدة بمجاحدة الجليّات حتى تأنس النفس بسماعها فيشك في اليقيني، كما أنه قد يتكرّر على سمعه ما ليس يقيناً من المحمودات فتذعن للتصديق به وتظنّ أنه يقيني بكثرة سماعه، وهذا أعظم مثارات الغلط ويعزّ في العقلاء من يحسن الاحتراز من الاغترار به.
فإن قلت: فمثل هذا اليقين عزيز يقل وجوده فتقل به المقدمات. قلنا: ما يتساعد فيه الوهم والعقل من الحسابيات والهندسيات والحسّيات كثير، فيكثر فيها مثل هذه اليقينيات وكذا المعقولات التي لا تحاذيها الوهميات. فأما العقليات الصرفة المتعلقة بالنظر في الإلهيات ففيها بعض مثل هذه اليقينيات، ولا يبلغ اليقين فيها إلى الحد الذي ذكرناه إلا بطول ممارسة العقليات وفطام العقل عن الوهميات والحسّيات وإيناسها بالعقليات المحضة، وكلما كان النظر فيها أكثر والجد في طلبها أتمّ كانت المعارف فيها إلى حدّ اليقين التام أقرب، ثم من طالت ممارسته وحصلت له ملكة بتلك المعارف لا يقدر على إفحام الخصم فيه ولا يقدر على تنزيل المسترشد منزلة نفسه بمجرد ذكر ما عنده إلا بأن يرشده إلى أن يسلك مسلكه في ممارسة العلوم وطول التأمل، حتى يصل إلى ما وصل إليه إن كان الحدس ثاقب العقل صافي الذكاء، وإن فارقه في الذكاء أو في الحدس أو تولّي الاعتبار الذي تولاه لم يصل إلى ما وصل إليه، وعند ذلك يقابل ما يحكيه نفسه بالإنكار ويشتغل بالتهجين والاستبعاد، وسبيل العارف البصير أن يعرض عنه صفحاً بل لا يبثّ إليه أسرار ما عنده فإن ذلك أسلم لجانبه وأقطع الشغب الجهال.
Interpretation (al-ta'wil) then doubt in the prophethood of the one from whom it is narrated, unlike what you understood if what you understood is certainty, for if you doubt his truthfulness, your certainty is not complete.
·
If you say: Perhaps a demonstration of his truthfulness appeared to me, then I heard from him what contradicts a demonstration that was established with me. I say: The existence of this is impossible, like the saying of one who says: If the mutawatir reports were contradictory, what would be the way in them, just as if it were mutawatir that Mecca exists and that it does not exist? This is impossible. So contradiction in demonstrations that include the conditions we have mentioned is impossible. If you see them contradictory, know that one of them or both did not fulfill the mentioned conditions. So examine the places of error and the seven sources that we detailed. Most error occurs in rushing to accept the premises of a demonstration as primary (awwaliyya), when they are not primary, but rather perhaps they are praiseworthy (mahmuda), well-known (mashhura), or imaginary (wahmiyya). It is not appropriate to accept premises unless certainty in them is at the limit we described. And just as one thinks that what is not primary is primary, so also one thinks that primaries are not primary, and thus doubts them. And one does not doubt primaries except by the mind departing from sound nature due to mixing with some bigoted theologians of corrupt sects who deny self-evident matters, until the soul becomes accustomed to hearing them and doubts the certain. Likewise, what is not certain from the praiseworthy propositions may be repeated on one's hearing, so one acquiesces to belief in it and thinks it is certain due to its frequent hearing. This is the greatest source of error, and it is rare among the rational people to guard well against being deceived by it.
If you say: Such certainty is rare, its existence is little, so premises become few with it. We say: That in which imagination and reason cooperate from arithmetic, geometric, and sensory matters is plentiful, so such certainties are plentiful in them, as are the intellectual matters that are not paralleled by imaginary ones. As for pure intellectual matters related to inquiry in divine things, some such certainties exist in them, but certainty in them does not reach the limit we mentioned except by long practice of intellectual matters, weaning the mind from imaginary and sensory matters, and familiarizing it with pure intellectual matters. The more the inquiry in them is prolonged and the effort in seeking them is complete, the closer the knowledge in them is to the limit of complete certainty. Then, one who has prolonged practice and has acquired a faculty with those knowledges cannot refute the opponent in it, nor can he bring the seeker down to his own level by merely stating what he has, except by guiding him to tread his path in practicing the sciences and long contemplation, until he reaches what he has reached, if the intuition (hads) is acute, the intellect is pure, and the intelligence is clear. If the seeker differs from him in intelligence or intuition or the mode of consideration that he undertook, he will not reach what he has reached. At that point, he meets what he narrates with denial and occupies himself with disparagement and deeming it far-fetched. The way of the knowledgeable insightful person is to turn away from him, indeed not to divulge to him the secrets of what he has, for that is safer for his side and more cut off from the clamor of the ignorant.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical and epistemological discussion addressing the possibility of contradiction between genuine demonstrations and the nature of certainty. The author responds to an objection: if a person has a demonstration for the truthfulness of a prophet, then hears something from that prophet that seems to contradict another demonstration, what should be done? The author argues that genuine contradictions among demonstrations that satisfy all conditions are impossible; apparent contradiction indicates a failure in the conditions. The main source of error is accepting premises as primary (awwaliyya) when they are not, such as well-known (mashhura) or imaginary (wahmiyya) propositions. One may also mistakenly doubt true primaries due to exposure to sophistical arguments from biased theologians. The author emphasizes that real certainty is attainable in mathematical and sensory matters, but in pure theology ('ilmiyyat) it requires extensive practice and weaning the mind from imagination. The passage concludes with advice: the wise person should not engage with those who refuse to follow the proper method of inquiry, as they will only resort to denial and disparagement.

The Greatest Source of Error: Confusing Premise Types
Author identifies the greatest source of error: confusing premise types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish primary (awwaliyya), well-known (mashhura), and imaginary (wahmiyya) premises, and identify the error of accepting the latter two as if they were certain.

Core Idea
Most errors in demonstration come from mistaking well-known or imaginary propositions for self-evident truths.

After establishing that contradiction is impossible in genuine demonstrations, the author warns us about the most common pitfall: accepting premises too quickly. He distinguishes between truly self-evident (awwaliyya) premises, which are immediately certain, and those that merely appear certain due to common acceptance (mashhura) or habit/imagination (wahmiyya). This lesson explores that distinction and its critical consequences for building sound arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three premise types mentioned
Find in the text: awwaliyya (primary), mahmuda mashhura (praiseworthy well-known), and wahmiyya (imaginary). Note that the author mentions them as sources of error.
The author groups mahmuda and mashhura together; in some texts they are distinguished.
2
Define each premise type
Awwaliyya: self-evident, no proof needed (e.g., 'the whole is greater than the part'). Mahmuda mashhura: widely accepted due to common opinion, but not certain (e.g., 'justice is good'). Wahmiyya: accepted due to imagination or habit, often false (e.g., 'every body has a color').
These definitions are based on the passage and standard logical terminology.
3
Understand the error of mistaken premises
The author says most error is 'rushing to accept premises as primary when they are not primary, but rather perhaps well-known or imaginary.' This error gives false certainty to arguments.
This is the core warning: don't automatically trust premises that feel obvious.
4
Recognize the complementary error
The author also notes that one might doubt true primaries due to exposure to sophistical arguments from biased theologians, which causes the mind to depart from its sound nature.
Both errors undermine certainty, but the first (mistaking non-primary for primary) is more common.
5
Apply the solution: check certainty level
The author advises: 'It is not appropriate to accept premises unless certainty in them is at the limit we described.' That limit is complete certainty, free from doubt and based on primary or derived premises.
One must verify the source of certainty before using a premise in demonstration.

Demonstration -- Burhan

The Three Components of a Demonstrative Science
Author identifies the three necessary components of any demonstrative science.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the three components (principles, subjects, questions) of any demonstrative science and apply them to Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Every demonstrative science is defined by its principles, subjects, and questions.

This passage introduces the foundational structure of any science that uses demonstration (burhan). The author defines three interconnected components: the principles (premises used in proofs), the subjects (the domain studied), and the questions (the conclusions to be proved). Understanding this tripartite division helps us analyze how Islamic disciplines like kalam, fiqh, and usul al-fiqh are organized and how they produce certain knowledge.

Guided Path
1
List the three components
The author explicitly states that demonstrations consist of three things: principles (مبادي), subjects (موضوعات), and questions (مسائل). Write them down in order.
2
Grasp the relational definitions
Each component is defined by its role in demonstration: subjects are that in which proofs are made, questions are that about which proofs are made, and principles are that by which proofs are made. Notice the prepositions: 'in', 'about', 'by'.
These relational definitions clarify the function of each part.
3
Identify principles as premises
The author equates principles (مبادي) with premises (مقدمات), which have been discussed previously. This means principles are not necessarily self-evident but include any accepted starting points.
Don't confuse with modern 'first principles'; here it's broader.
4
Observe the examples of subjects
The author lists four sciences and their subjects: geometry (magnitude), arithmetic (number), physics (natural body in motion/rest), grammar (Arabic language). Note that each subject defines the scope of that science.
The passage cuts off mid-sentence; more examples may follow.
5
Apply to an Islamic science
Choose an Islamic science (e.g., usul al-fiqh, hadith criticism, tafsir) and identify its subject, questions, and principles. For instance, in usul al-fiqh, the subject is the evidence (adilla) from which rulings are derived; the questions are the rulings themselves; the principles include axioms like 'certainty is not removed by doubt'.
This task solidifies the concepts.

Commentary
This passage introduces the three components that make up a demonstrative science (برهان). The author first enumerates them: principles (مبادي), subjects (موضوعات), and questions (مسائل). He defines them relationally: subjects are the domain within which proofs occur, questions are the conclusions to be proved, and principles are the premises used to prove. Principles refer to the axioms or previously established premises. Subjects are the foundational entities of each science; the science investigates their essential accidents ('essential' in the second sense from the previous chapter, where the subject is part of the predicate's definition). The author then gives examples: geometry studies magnitude, arithmetic studies number, physics studies the natural body with respect to motion and rest, and grammar studies Arabic language (the sentence cuts off). This classification is key to understanding how different sciences are structured and how demonstrations operate within them.

The Three Components of a Demonstration
Author enumerates the three components of demonstrations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the three components (principles, subjects, problems) of any demonstration in an Islamic science.

Core Idea
Every demonstration rests on three pillars: principles, subjects, and problems.

In this passage, the author introduces the three essential components that make up a demonstration (burhan). Understanding these components is crucial for analyzing how proofs are constructed in Islamic sciences such as kalam, fiqh, and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three components
Read the first two lines. The author explicitly states that demonstrations consist of three things: principles (mabadi'), subjects (mawdu'at), and problems (masa'il).
2
Understand the definitions
Note the definitions: subjects are that in which demonstration is made (the subject matter of the science), problems are that upon which demonstration is made (the propositions to be proved), and principles are that by which demonstration is made (the premises).
3
Map the components to a real example
Take the example: In the science of kalam, the subject is the world, the problem is 'the world is temporally originated', and the principles are premises like 'every temporally originated thing has a cause'.
This example is derived from the passage's logic, though not explicitly stated.

فصل فيما يلتئم به أمر البراهين وهي ثلاثة : مبادي وموضوعات و مسائل. فالموضوعات نعني بها ما يبرهن فيها، والمسائل ما يبرهن عليها والمبادي ما يبرهن بها. والمراد بالمبادي المقدمات، وقد ذكرناها . وأما الموضوعات فهي الأمور التي توضع في العلوم وتطلب أعراضها الذاتية، أعني الذاتية بالمعنى الثاني من المعنيين المذكورين، ولكلّ علم موضوع؛ فموضوع الهندسة المقدار، وموضوع الحساب العدد، وموضوع العلم الملقب بالطبيعي جسم العالم من جهة ما يتحرك ويسكن وموضوع النحو لغة العرب من جهة ما يختلف إعرابها وموضوع الفقه أفعال المكلفين من جهة ما يُنهى عنها أو يُؤمر بها أو يُباح أو يُندب أو يُكره، وموضوع أصول الفقه أحكام الشرع، أعني الوجوب والحظر والإباحة من جهة ما تدرك به من أدلتها وموضوع المنطق تمييز المعقولات وتلخيص المعاني . وأما المسائل فهي القضايا الخاصة بكل علم التي تُطلب المعرفة في العلوم بأحد طرفيها : إما النفي وإما الإثبات كقولنا في الحساب : هذا العدد إما زوج أو فرد، وفي الهندسة : هذا المقدار مساو أو مُباين وفي الفقه : هذا الفعل حلال أو حرام أو واجب، وفي العلم الإلهي : هذا الموجود قديم أو حادث وهذا الموجود له سبب أو ليس له سبب . والمقصود أن محمول المسائل إن كان مطلوباً بالنظر فلا يجوز أن يكون ذاتياً للموضوع بالمعنى الأول، لأنه إذا كان كذلك كان معلوماً قبل العلم بالموضوع فإن الحيوان الذي هو ذاتي للإنسان بمعنى أنه وجد في حده لا يجوز أن يكون مطلوباً، فإن من عرف الإنسان فقد عرف كونه حيواناً قبله لا محالة، فإن أجزاء الحدّ يتقدم العلم بها على العلم بالمحدود، ولكن الذاتي بالمعنى الثاني وهو المطلوب وأما كلّ محمول ليس بالمعنى الثاني ولا بالمعنى الأول فإنه يُسمّى غريباً كقولنا في الهندسة عند النظر في الخطوط : هذا الخط حسن أو قبيح، لأن الحسن والقبح لا يؤخذ في حد الخط ولا الخط في حدّه، بل الذاتي لذاته مستقيم أو مُنْحَنِ وأمثاله . وكذا قولنا في الطب : هذا الجرح مستدير أو مربع، فإنه محمول غريب للجرح إذ لا يؤخذ واحد منهما في حدّ الآخر، وإنما هو ذاتي للأشكال. وقد يكون المحمول ذاتياً للموضوع بالمعنى الثاني، ولكن يكون غريباً بالإضافة إلى العلم الذي يستعمل فيه كقولنا في الفقه هذه الحركة سريعة أو بطيئة، فإن السرعة والبطء ذاتي للحركة، ولكن إنما يطلب في العلم الطبيعي. والمطلوب في الفقه ذاتي آخر وهو كونه واجباً أو محظوراً أو مباحاً . وإذا قلنا في العلم الطبيعي : هذا الفعل حلال أو حرام، كان غريباً من العلم . فإن قيل : فهل يجوز أن يكون المحمول في المقدمتين ذاتياً بالمعنى الأول؟ قلنا : لا ، لأنه إن كان كذلك تكون النتيجة معلومة، فإذا قلنا : الإنسان حيوان والحيوان جسم، فالإنسان جسم. فالعلم بالنتيجة غير مطلوب فإن من عرف الإنسان فقد عرف أجزاء حدّه جميعاً وهي الجسم والحيوان. نعم لا يبعد أن يكون كلّ واحدٍ ذاتياً بالمعنى الثاني، بل إن كان أحدهما ذاتياً
A section on what constitutes the matter of demonstrations. They are three: principles, subjects, and problems. By 'subjects' we mean that in which demonstration is made; by 'problems' that upon which demonstration is made; and by 'principles' that by which demonstration is made. And by 'principles' is meant the premises, which we have already mentioned. As for subjects, they are the things that are posited in the sciences and whose essential attributes are sought, meaning the essential in the second of the two mentioned senses. And every science has a subject: the subject of geometry is magnitude; the subject of arithmetic is number; the subject of the science called natural is the body of the world insofar as it moves and rests; the subject of grammar is the language of the Arabs insofar as its inflection varies; the subject of fiqh is the actions of legally responsible persons insofar as they are prohibited, commanded, permissible, recommended, or disliked; the subject of usul al-fiqh is the rulings of the Sharia, i.e., obligation, prohibition, and permission, insofar as they are known from their evidences; and the subject of logic is the distinguishing of intelligibles and the distillation of meanings. As for problems, they are the particular propositions of each science whose knowledge is sought in the sciences with either of its two sides: either negation or affirmation, like our statement in arithmetic: this number is either even or odd; in geometry: this magnitude is equal or different; in fiqh: this action is permissible or forbidden or obligatory; in divine science: this existent is eternal or temporally originated, and this existent has a cause or has no cause. And the intended meaning is that the predicate of a problem, if sought by investigation, is not allowed to be essential to the subject in the first sense, because if it were so, it would be known prior to knowledge of the subject. For animal, which is essential to man in the sense that it is found in his definition, is not allowed to be sought, because whoever knows man has necessarily known that he is an animal before it, since the parts of the definition are known prior to the definition. But essential in the second sense is what is sought. And every predicate that is neither in the second sense nor in the first is called 'strange' (gharib), like our statement in geometry when considering lines: this line is beautiful or ugly, because beauty and ugliness are not taken in the definition of line, nor the line in their definition; rather, what is essential to it is straight or curved and the like. Similarly, our statement in medicine: this wound is round or square, for it is a strange predicate for the wound since neither is taken in the definition of the other; rather, it is essential to shapes. And it is possible that the predicate is essential to the subject in the second sense, but it is strange relative to the science in which it is used, like our statement in fiqh: this movement is fast or slow, for speed and slowness are essential to movement, but they are sought only in natural science. And what is sought in fiqh is another essential, namely it being obligatory, prohibited, or permissible. And if we say in natural science: this action is permissible or forbidden, it is strange to that science. If it is said: is it permissible for the predicate in the two premises to be essential in the first sense? We say: no, because if it were so, the conclusion would be known. For if we say: Man is an animal, and animal is a body, so man is a body. The knowledge of the conclusion is not sought, because whoever knows man has known all the parts of his definition, namely body and animal. Indeed, it is not far that each one is essential in the second sense, but rather if one of them is essential...
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage begins by stating that demonstrations are composed of three things: principles (premises), subjects (the subject matter of the science), and problems (the propositions to be proved). The author then defines each. The subject of a science is the entity whose essential attributes (in the second sense of essential, where the subject is part of the predicate's definition) are investigated. He gives examples for many sciences: geometry (magnitude), arithmetic (number), natural science (the body of the world), grammar (Arabic), fiqh (actions of mukallafin), usul al-fiqh (Sharia rulings), and logic (distinguishing intelligibles). Then he defines problems as the particular propositions of each science, whose truth is sought, typically either affirmative or negative. He then discusses the predicate of a problem: it cannot be essential in the first sense (i.e., part of the subject's definition) because that would be known beforehand. It must be essential in the second sense (i.e., the subject is part of its definition) or else it is 'strange' (gharib), like aesthetics in geometry or shape in medicine. The passage also notes that a predicate might be essential in the second sense for a certain subject but strange when used outside its proper science. Finally, a question is raised: can the predicate in the premises be essential in the first sense? The answer is no, because that would make the conclusion already known, as in the example 'man is animal, animal is body' gives the conclusion 'man is body', but man's definition already includes body and animal, so the conclusion is not sought. However, it is not impossible that each predicate is essential in the second sense.

Key Terms
المبادي (al-mabadi'): The premises used in demonstrations. [Note: Often includes axioms and postulates; here used broadly for premises.]
الموضوعات (al-mawdu'at): The things that are posited in a science and whose essential attributes (in the second sense of essential) are sought. [Note: The term 'mawdu' is used consistently in Peripatetic logic for the subject-matter of a science; stable.]
المسائل (al-masa'il): The particular propositions of a science whose truth is sought (affirmation or negation). [Note: Stable as the 'questions' or 'theorems' of a science.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what are the three components of a demonstration?
A) Subjects, predicates, and conclusions
B) Principles, subjects, and problems
C) Premises, terms, and syllogisms
D) Definitions, axioms, and postulates

Key Takeaway
A demonstration requires three distinct elements: what it is about (subject), what is to be proved (problem), and what it is proved from (principles).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Subjects of the Islamic Sciences
Author defines subject and lists subjects of various sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject (mawdu') of any Islamic science and explain the meaning of 'essential attributes in the second sense'.

Core Idea
Each Islamic science has a distinct subject that defines the scope of its inquiry.

The author illustrates how each science has a specific subject matter, such as magnitude for geometry or actions of mukallafin for fiqh. Understanding the subject of a science is key to knowing what essential properties are studied.

Guided Path
1
List the sciences and their subjects
Read the list: geometry (magnitude), arithmetic (number), natural science (body of the world), grammar (Arabic), fiqh (actions), usul al-fiqh (Sharia rulings), logic (intelligibles).
Memorize these for reference.
2
Understand 'essential in the second sense'
The author says the subject's essential attributes are sought, meaning predicates that include the subject in their definition. Example: 'animal' is essential to man in the first sense, but 'rational' is essential in the second sense.
The text refers to previous discussion of two senses.
3
Apply to an unfamiliar science
If you encounter a new Islamic science, deduce its subject from its definitions or examples. For instance, the science of hadith has as its subject the transmitted reports from the Prophet.

A section on what constitutes the matter of demonstrations. They are three: principles, subjects, and problems. By 'subjects' we mean that in which demonstration is made; by 'problems' that upon which demonstration is made; and by 'principles' that by which demonstration is made. And by 'principles' is meant the premises, which we have already mentioned. As for subjects, they are the things that are posited in the sciences and whose essential attributes are sought, meaning the essential in the second of the two mentioned senses. And every science has a subject: the subject of geometry is magnitude; the subject of arithmetic is number; the subject of the science called natural is the body of the world insofar as it moves and rests; the subject of grammar is the language of the Arabs insofar as its inflection varies; the subject of fiqh is the actions of legally responsible persons insofar as they are prohibited, commanded, permissible, recommended, or disliked; the subject of usul al-fiqh is the rulings of the Sharia, i.e., obligation, prohibition, and permission, insofar as they are known from their evidences; and the subject of logic is the distinguishing of intelligibles and the distillation of meanings. As for problems, they are the particular propositions of each science whose knowledge is sought in the sciences with either of its two sides: either negation or affirmation, like our statement in arithmetic: this number is either even or odd; in geometry: this magnitude is equal or different; in fiqh: this action is permissible or forbidden or obligatory; in divine science: this existent is eternal or temporally originated, and this existent has a cause or has no cause. And the intended meaning is that the predicate of a problem, if sought by investigation, is not allowed to be essential to the subject in the first sense, because if it were so, it would be known prior to knowledge of the subject. For animal, which is essential to man in the sense that it is found in his definition, is not allowed to be sought, because whoever knows man has necessarily known that he is an animal before it, since the parts of the definition are known prior to the definition. But essential in the second sense is what is sought. And every predicate that is neither in the second sense nor in the first is called 'strange' (gharib), like our statement in geometry when considering lines: this line is beautiful or ugly, because beauty and ugliness are not taken in the definition of line, nor the line in their definition; rather, what is essential to it is straight or curved and the like. Similarly, our statement in medicine: this wound is round or square, for it is a strange predicate for the wound since neither is taken in the definition of the other; rather, it is essential to shapes. And it is possible that the predicate is essential to the subject in the second sense, but it is strange relative to the science in which it is used, like our statement in fiqh: this movement is fast or slow, for speed and slowness are essential to movement, but they are sought only in natural science. And what is sought in fiqh is another essential, namely it being obligatory, prohibited, or permissible. And if we say in natural science: this action is permissible or forbidden, it is strange to that science. If it is said: is it permissible for the predicate in the two premises to be essential in the first sense? We say: no, because if it were so, the conclusion would be known. For if we say: Man is an animal, and animal is a body, so man is a body. The knowledge of the conclusion is not sought, because whoever knows man has known all the parts of his definition, namely body and animal. Indeed, it is not far that each one is essential in the second sense, but rather if one of them is essential...
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage begins by stating that demonstrations are composed of three things: principles (premises), subjects (the subject matter of the science), and problems (the propositions to be proved). The author then defines each. The subject of a science is the entity whose essential attributes (in the second sense of essential, where the subject is part of the predicate's definition) are investigated. He gives examples for many sciences: geometry (magnitude), arithmetic (number), natural science (the body of the world), grammar (Arabic), fiqh (actions of mukallafin), usul al-fiqh (Sharia rulings), and logic (distinguishing intelligibles). Then he defines problems as the particular propositions of each science, whose truth is sought, typically either affirmative or negative. He then discusses the predicate of a problem: it cannot be essential in the first sense (i.e., part of the subject's definition) because that would be known beforehand. It must be essential in the second sense (i.e., the subject is part of its definition) or else it is 'strange' (gharib), like aesthetics in geometry or shape in medicine. The passage also notes that a predicate might be essential in the second sense for a certain subject but strange when used outside its proper science. Finally, a question is raised: can the predicate in the premises be essential in the first sense? The answer is no, because that would make the conclusion already known, as in the example 'man is animal, animal is body' gives the conclusion 'man is body', but man's definition already includes body and animal, so the conclusion is not sought. However, it is not impossible that each predicate is essential in the second sense.

Key Terms
الموضوعات (al-mawdu'at): The things that are posited in a science and whose essential attributes (in the second sense of essential) are sought. [Note: The term 'mawdu' is used consistently in Peripatetic logic for the subject-matter of a science; stable.]
الذاتية بالمعنى الثاني (al-dhatiyya bi al-ma'na al-thani): Essential in the second sense: the subject is part of the predicate's definition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) The meanings of words
D) The science of literature

Key Takeaway
The subject of a science is the entity whose essential properties are studied, and it must be defined precisely to avoid overlap with other sciences.

Demonstration -- Burhan

Problems and Predicates: Essential vs. Strange
Author discusses the nature of predicates in problems.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a predicate in a scientific proposition as essential (in the second sense) or strange (gharib) relative to the subject and science.

Core Idea
The predicate of a problem must be essential in the second sense, not in the first, else the problem is already known.

The author explains what qualifies as a proper predicate for a problem in a science. If the predicate is part of the subject's definition (first sense), the proposition is trivial. It must be an essential attribute in the second sense (subject part of predicate's definition) or else it is considered 'strange' (gharib) to that science.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two senses of essential
First sense: the predicate is part of the subject's definition (e.g., 'animal' for man). Second sense: the subject is part of the predicate's definition (e.g., 'rational' for man).
2
Analyze the condition for problem predicates
The predicate of a problem cannot be essential in the first sense, because that would make the proposition already known. It must be essential in the second sense or else it is considered gharib.
3
Identify examples of gharib predicates
Example: 'This line is beautiful' in geometry. Beauty is not in the definition of line, nor line in the definition of beauty. So it's strange to geometry.
Other examples from fiqh and medicine are given.
4
Apply the criterion to a new example
Take the proposition 'This act of prayer is valid'. Is 'valid' essential in the second sense to the subject 'act of prayer'? Probably yes, because validity is defined in terms of the act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل فيما يلتئم به أمر البراهين وهي ثلاثة : مبادي وموضوعات و مسائل. فالموضوعات نعني بها ما يبرهن فيها، والمسائل ما يبرهن عليها والمبادي ما يبرهن بها. والمراد بالمبادي المقدمات، وقد ذكرناها . وأما الموضوعات فهي الأمور التي توضع في العلوم وتطلب أعراضها الذاتية، أعني الذاتية بالمعنى الثاني من المعنيين المذكورين، ولكلّ علم موضوع؛ فموضوع الهندسة المقدار، وموضوع الحساب العدد، وموضوع العلم الملقب بالطبيعي جسم العالم من جهة ما يتحرك ويسكن وموضوع النحو لغة العرب من جهة ما يختلف إعرابها وموضوع الفقه أفعال المكلفين من جهة ما يُنهى عنها أو يُؤمر بها أو يُباح أو يُندب أو يُكره، وموضوع أصول الفقه أحكام الشرع، أعني الوجوب والحظر والإباحة من جهة ما تدرك به من أدلتها وموضوع المنطق تمييز المعقولات وتلخيص المعاني . وأما المسائل فهي القضايا الخاصة بكل علم التي تُطلب المعرفة في العلوم بأحد طرفيها : إما النفي وإما الإثبات كقولنا في الحساب : هذا العدد إما زوج أو فرد، وفي الهندسة : هذا المقدار مساو أو مُباين وفي الفقه : هذا الفعل حلال أو حرام أو واجب، وفي العلم الإلهي : هذا الموجود قديم أو حادث وهذا الموجود له سبب أو ليس له سبب . والمقصود أن محمول المسائل إن كان مطلوباً بالنظر فلا يجوز أن يكون ذاتياً للموضوع بالمعنى الأول، لأنه إذا كان كذلك كان معلوماً قبل العلم بالموضوع فإن الحيوان الذي هو ذاتي للإنسان بمعنى أنه وجد في حده لا يجوز أن يكون مطلوباً، فإن من عرف الإنسان فقد عرف كونه حيواناً قبله لا محالة، فإن أجزاء الحدّ يتقدم العلم بها على العلم بالمحدود، ولكن الذاتي بالمعنى الثاني وهو المطلوب وأما كلّ محمول ليس بالمعنى الثاني ولا بالمعنى الأول فإنه يُسمّى غريباً كقولنا في الهندسة عند النظر في الخطوط : هذا الخط حسن أو قبيح، لأن الحسن والقبح لا يؤخذ في حد الخط ولا الخط في حدّه، بل الذاتي لذاته مستقيم أو مُنْحَنِ وأمثاله . وكذا قولنا في الطب : هذا الجرح مستدير أو مربع، فإنه محمول غريب للجرح إذ لا يؤخذ واحد منهما في حدّ الآخر، وإنما هو ذاتي للأشكال. وقد يكون المحمول ذاتياً للموضوع بالمعنى الثاني، ولكن يكون غريباً بالإضافة إلى العلم الذي يستعمل فيه كقولنا في الفقه هذه الحركة سريعة أو بطيئة، فإن السرعة والبطء ذاتي للحركة، ولكن إنما يطلب في العلم الطبيعي. والمطلوب في الفقه ذاتي آخر وهو كونه واجباً أو محظوراً أو مباحاً . وإذا قلنا في العلم الطبيعي : هذا الفعل حلال أو حرام، كان غريباً من العلم . فإن قيل : فهل يجوز أن يكون المحمول في المقدمتين ذاتياً بالمعنى الأول؟ قلنا : لا ، لأنه إن كان كذلك تكون النتيجة معلومة، فإذا قلنا : الإنسان حيوان والحيوان جسم، فالإنسان جسم. فالعلم بالنتيجة غير مطلوب فإن من عرف الإنسان فقد عرف أجزاء حدّه جميعاً وهي الجسم والحيوان. نعم لا يبعد أن يكون كلّ واحدٍ ذاتياً بالمعنى الثاني، بل إن كان أحدهما ذاتياً
A section on what constitutes the matter of demonstrations. They are three: principles, subjects, and problems. By 'subjects' we mean that in which demonstration is made; by 'problems' that upon which demonstration is made; and by 'principles' that by which demonstration is made. And by 'principles' is meant the premises, which we have already mentioned. As for subjects, they are the things that are posited in the sciences and whose essential attributes are sought, meaning the essential in the second of the two mentioned senses. And every science has a subject: the subject of geometry is magnitude; the subject of arithmetic is number; the subject of the science called natural is the body of the world insofar as it moves and rests; the subject of grammar is the language of the Arabs insofar as its inflection varies; the subject of fiqh is the actions of legally responsible persons insofar as they are prohibited, commanded, permissible, recommended, or disliked; the subject of usul al-fiqh is the rulings of the Sharia, i.e., obligation, prohibition, and permission, insofar as they are known from their evidences; and the subject of logic is the distinguishing of intelligibles and the distillation of meanings. As for problems, they are the particular propositions of each science whose knowledge is sought in the sciences with either of its two sides: either negation or affirmation, like our statement in arithmetic: this number is either even or odd; in geometry: this magnitude is equal or different; in fiqh: this action is permissible or forbidden or obligatory; in divine science: this existent is eternal or temporally originated, and this existent has a cause or has no cause. And the intended meaning is that the predicate of a problem, if sought by investigation, is not allowed to be essential to the subject in the first sense, because if it were so, it would be known prior to knowledge of the subject. For animal, which is essential to man in the sense that it is found in his definition, is not allowed to be sought, because whoever knows man has necessarily known that he is an animal before it, since the parts of the definition are known prior to the definition. But essential in the second sense is what is sought. And every predicate that is neither in the second sense nor in the first is called 'strange' (gharib), like our statement in geometry when considering lines: this line is beautiful or ugly, because beauty and ugliness are not taken in the definition of line, nor the line in their definition; rather, what is essential to it is straight or curved and the like. Similarly, our statement in medicine: this wound is round or square, for it is a strange predicate for the wound since neither is taken in the definition of the other; rather, it is essential to shapes. And it is possible that the predicate is essential to the subject in the second sense, but it is strange relative to the science in which it is used, like our statement in fiqh: this movement is fast or slow, for speed and slowness are essential to movement, but they are sought only in natural science. And what is sought in fiqh is another essential, namely it being obligatory, prohibited, or permissible. And if we say in natural science: this action is permissible or forbidden, it is strange to that science. If it is said: is it permissible for the predicate in the two premises to be essential in the first sense? We say: no, because if it were so, the conclusion would be known. For if we say: Man is an animal, and animal is a body, so man is a body. The knowledge of the conclusion is not sought, because whoever knows man has known all the parts of his definition, namely body and animal. Indeed, it is not far that each one is essential in the second sense, but rather if one of them is essential...
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage begins by stating that demonstrations are composed of three things: principles (premises), subjects (the subject matter of the science), and problems (the propositions to be proved). The author then defines each. The subject of a science is the entity whose essential attributes (in the second sense of essential, where the subject is part of the predicate's definition) are investigated. He gives examples for many sciences: geometry (magnitude), arithmetic (number), natural science (the body of the world), grammar (Arabic), fiqh (actions of mukallafin), usul al-fiqh (Sharia rulings), and logic (distinguishing intelligibles). Then he defines problems as the particular propositions of each science, whose truth is sought, typically either affirmative or negative. He then discusses the predicate of a problem: it cannot be essential in the first sense (i.e., part of the subject's definition) because that would be known beforehand. It must be essential in the second sense (i.e., the subject is part of its definition) or else it is 'strange' (gharib), like aesthetics in geometry or shape in medicine. The passage also notes that a predicate might be essential in the second sense for a certain subject but strange when used outside its proper science. Finally, a question is raised: can the predicate in the premises be essential in the first sense? The answer is no, because that would make the conclusion already known, as in the example 'man is animal, animal is body' gives the conclusion 'man is body', but man's definition already includes body and animal, so the conclusion is not sought. Key Terms
المسائل (al-masa'il): The particular propositions of a science whose truth is sought (affirmation or negation). [Note: Stable as the 'questions' or 'theorems' of a science.]
الذاتي بالمعنى الأول (al-dhati bi al-ma'na al-awwal): Essential in the first sense: the predicate is part of the subject's definition.
الذاتي بالمعنى الثاني (al-dhati bi al-ma'na al-thani): Essential in the second sense: the subject is part of the predicate's definition.
الغريب (al-gharib): A predicate that is neither essential in the first sense nor in the second sense relative to the subject. [Note: Sometimes called 'accidental' or 'extraneous'; stable.]

Key Takeaway
To be a genuine problem in a science, the predicate must be essential to the subject in the second sense; otherwise it is either trivial or foreign to that science.

Definition and Criteria of True Demonstration (Burhān Ḥaqīqī)
Author defines true demonstration as providing necessary eternal certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the essential epistemic criteria of true demonstration as defined by the author and distinguish it from other types of proof.

Core Idea
True demonstration yields necessary, eternal, immutable certainty.

This lesson introduces the foundational concept of burhān ḥaqīqī, the highest form of proof in Islamic logic. Understanding its criteria is essential for evaluating arguments in theology and jurisprudence. The passage defines true demonstration and gives concrete examples from kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Locate the term being defined in the Arabic. The author defines 'al-burhān al-ḥaqīqī' (true demonstration).
The definiendum is the subject of the definition.
2
Extract the epistemic criteria
The definition specifies that true demonstration yields 'al-yaqīn al-ḍarūrī al-dāʼim al-abadī' — necessary, perpetual, eternal certainty that is impossible to change.
These criteria are the differentia that distinguish burhān ḥaqīqī from other forms of argument.
3
Recognize the examples
The author provides two examples: knowledge that the world is temporally originated (ḥādith) and that it has a creator (ṣāniʻ). These illustrate truths that are eternally impossible to be otherwise.
Examples come from kalam, grounding the definition in Islamic theological discourse.
4
Understand the negation of change
The passage emphasizes that it is impossible for a time to come when these truths could be judged differently. This underscores the absolute permanence of the certainty produced.
This rules out any form of probable or revisable knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل فيما يقوم فيه البرهان الحقيقي اعلم أن البرهان الحقيقي ما يفيد اليقين الضروري الدائم الأبدي الذي يستحيل تغييره كعلمك بأن العالم حادث وأن له صانعاً، وأمثال ذلك مما يستحيل أن يكون بخلافه على الأبد، إذ يستحيل أن يحضرنا زمان نحكم فيه على العالم
Section on what constitutes true demonstration. Know that true demonstration is that which yields necessary, perpetual, eternal certainty that is impossible to change, like your knowledge that the world is temporally originated and that it has a Maker, and similar things that are eternally impossible to be otherwise, for it is impossible that a time should come when we judge the world...
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage provides a definition of true demonstration (al-burhān al-ḥaqīqī). The author states that true demonstration is what produces necessary, perpetual, eternal certainty that cannot change. Two examples are given: knowledge that the world is temporally originated (ḥādith) and that it has a Creator (ṣāniʿ). These are truths that are eternally impossible to be otherwise. The phrase 'for it is impossible that a time should come when we judge the world...' is cut off, but the meaning is clear: the certainty is such that no future time could make it false. This definition emphasizes that burhān ḥaqīqī is not just any certain argument, but one with absolute necessity and permanence, as opposed to dialectical or rhetorical arguments that might be probable or persuasive only.

Key Terms
البرهان الحقيقي (al-burhān al-ḥaqīqī): True demonstration that yields necessary, perpetual, eternal certainty that is immutable, such as knowledge that the world is temporally originated and has a Maker. [Note: This definition is standard in the Islamic logical tradition, following Aristotle's Posterior Analytics; stable.]
اليقين الضروري (al-yaqīn al-ḍarūrī): Necessary certainty that is not subject to doubt or change; it is the epistemic result of true demonstration. [Note: Stable term across epistemology and logic.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
True demonstration (burhān ḥaqīqī) is defined as that which yields necessary, perpetual, eternal certainty that is impossible to change.
